Breaking down football for swifties. Welcome to football. We're stoked to have you.   1) A FG is a kick through the upright goal posts, usually on fourth down (fourth try to get ten yards before the defense gets the ball back) and the farthest FG ever hit was done by Baltimore Ravens kicker Justin Tucker at 66 yards.   2) Travis blocks and catches the ball. Hes basically a big hybrid Wide Receiver. If the Quarterback is handing the ball to the Runningback on a run play, Travis would block defenders trying to get to whoever has the ball on offense. The guy giving the ball to the QB on every snap (play/down/offensive snap) is the center (of the O Line, and that's the position Jason plays)  3. A down is one of four tries to get ten yards. Usually if the offense doesnt get ten yards in three tries they either attempt a field goal if theyre <60 yards from the goal post/uprights. If they are farther than that the punter comes in and kicks the ball to a punt returner who decides where his teams offense will take over. If the punting/kicking team misses, or fails when trying to get the last little bit of yards necessary to get a new set of downs, it is called a turniver on downs and the other teams offense takes the ball and tries to move it the other way into their end zone for a TD.   4. You can follow it on the NFL app or any of the other sports websites. Just google your team and put scores and/or play by play and you should find a good option. NFL games are on Thursday nights, Monday nights, and all day Sunday (usually). There are also usually three games on Thanksgiving. College Football is on Saturdays. Fox, CBS, NBC.  5. Tight End has become like a hybrid wide receiver. Big, fast, tall guys that can out muscle and out jump smaller, faster Defensive Backs, and LineBackers. They block (help the Offensive Line) on Running plays. Great targets for within twenty yards of the end zone (known as the Red Zone)  6. Corner backs usually follow receivers or stay closer to the Front 7 (D Line, and Linebackers) to assist in tackling ballcarriers close to the line of scrimmage. Safeties are essentially a safety blanket for the defense. They can come up and help in the run game but that leaves the dedense vulnerable to deep routes by the WRs, or less TD prevention of the ball carrier gets past the front 7 of the defense.  Honorable Mentions:  1) a Field Goal is worth three points. A Touchdown is worth six (and get a shot at an extra point, short field goal, or go for a two point conversion)  2) the defense can also get the ball an interception (they catch the pass from the opposing offense, usually the QB) or a fumble (when the ballcarrier loses the ball and the defense gets it)   3) Regular penalties youll see the refs call (and you'll learn to despise them and blame your teams inadequacies on them):  a) Pass Interference: Can go on offense or defense. Usually goes on defense on a CB/DB. It's when (>5 yards from the LoS) when they stop the WR from having a fair shot at the catch (early tackle, not playing the ball), and offensive pass interference doesnt happen as often but its when the WR stops the DB from making a play on a bad throw/potential Interception before the ball gets there.   b) Holding: Holding an opponents jersey to stop them from advancing towards the QB or through a block on the DL (usually happens on the OL)  c) False Start: offensive line moves before the ball is snapped after everyone is set.   Happens to the defense too but is usually called encroachment or neutral zone infraction if they cross the line of scrimmage.    4) On offense a player is down when a limb other than their hand, head, or foot touch the ground with the ball. Usually a leg, knee, shoulder, or elbow. If the ballcarrier falls down they can get up and run again unless the oposing team touches them down.   Also, forgot to add that Defensive Pass Interference is a spot foul which is HUGE for the offense because if the QB hurls the ball 50 yards to his WR/TE/RB and DPI is called it's the same as a huge completion and the ball moves to wear the foul occured. It usually happens more than 10 yards down the field which usually leads to a new set of downs for the offense. Not necessarily fair considering OPI is a ten yard penalty but the new age of NFL rules favors the offense.  Also, a caveat to the last thing I mentioned, if the ball carrier looks like he's down but doesn't hear a whistle and is on top of a defender that's on the ground the play is still live as long as his knee/elbow/shoulder/back doesn't touch the ground while he has possession of the ball. If you see players stop because they think the ball carrier is down but the whistle hasn't been blown the ball carrier can keep going for the TD.  I'm a Seahawks fan that fell in love with the game of football after our SB run in 2005 where the refs stole the ring from us and gave it to Pittsburgh as a going away/retiring present for Jerome Bettis. Yes I'm still salty about it. My team went 13-3 and had the league MVP at RB and lost to a 11-5 Pittsburgh team that lost their way to a SB. #GoHawks  My wife is a Swifty and I'm excited to be able to talk to her about football and see her get into it now too. Especially since it's not the f***in Kardashians or something like that. I say the NFL is my soaps and I'm not even exaggerating a little bit.   Welcome to the game we know and love dearly,  -Sven

For those interested in the backstory of the Browns bottlegate incident referenced here: In 2001, the Browns were 6-6 and fighting to try to make the playoffs for the 1st time since returning to the league in '99. Late in the season, they were playing the Jaguars in Cleveland and the Browns were trailing 15-10 late in the game with under 2 mins to go, but they were driving down the field and were in Jags territory. A 4th down came up and the Browns went for it and Couch hit Quincy Morgan for the 1st down. The Browns then rushed to the line and spiked the ball to stop the clock. However, the refs said they got notified before the spike that the booth wanted to review the 4th down pass to Morgan to see if he actually caught it and so they proceeded to review the play and overturned the call on the field and gave the ball to the Jags. The thing that pissed off the fans was the fact that another play had already been ran (the spike) and you're not allowed to go back two plays to review a previous play. The fans got rightfully pissed and started throwing beer bottles and debris on the field and at the refs. The refs called the game and got pelted as they ran into the tunnel.  Another note here: earlier that same season, the Browns were playing the Bears and had a lead late in the game with under 2 mins to go. The booth signaled they wanted to review a play but another play had already been run so the refs said by rule they couldn't go back to review the play that triggered the potential review. The reviews of said play showed that had they been allowed to review, the call would have went in the Browns favor and they probably hang onto win that game. They lost that game after giving up a TD, onside kick, a hail mary TD to tie the game in regulation (were up 14 with under 2 mins to go), and a tipped pass that went for a pick 6 in OT.   So basically, Browns were screwed by the refs in similar scenarios. 1 where they didn't allow the booth review of a previous play and 1 where they did allow it despite another play having been run.

RE: wide right  I could tell by the way the kicker struck the ball, as well as by the way it pushed right...  the wind was dramatically different at about the ten yard line than it was at the spot of the kick.  If you watch closely, it was obvious that the ball was tracking *perfectly* , given the distance and hash mark - until it got to the ten yard line.    You can argue that it was the kicker's home field.  He should be familiar with the conditions and wind at both ends, and adjust accordingly.  I'm sure he feels that way.  But, it's very difficult to judge differences in wind velocity and direction without visual reference - which he did not have.  I was supporting the Chiefs, but I felt horrible for that kicker.
